Varieties:

The 1928 halfcrown has the reverse design, first introduced in the proof set of 1927. This year sees three major varieties. There are two obverses found (obverse 1 and obverse 2) and two reverses (reverse B and reverse C). However, the die pairs are not fully meshed, with pairings 1+B, 2+B and 1+C found. Davies catalogues these three types with numbers 1701, 1702 and 1703, respectively.

On obverse 1, the king has an idented shallow forehead.
On obverse 2, the king has a flat full forehead.

On reverse B, there are 157 border beads and the tip of the '9' in the date ends over a bead.
On reverse C, there are 158 border beads and the tip of the '9' in the date ends over a space between beads.

-

Queen Mary and Princess Elizabeth are standing to the left with the house visible behind them. King George V is standing to the right, bending down to speak to his Cairn Terrier, Snip. This photograph was taken in September 1928, probably by the Duke of York, later King George VI. He took many informal photographs of his young daughters Princess Elizabeth and Princess Margaret before his accession to the throne in 1936.

Varieties:

Note:

The Coinage Act of 1873 demonetised silver, leading to the demise of the silver dollar. Although "trade" dollars were produced from 1873 to 1878, they were meant for circulation overseas and were not legal tender in America. In response to the sour economic condition that persisted through much of the 1870s, and as a sop to Western suppliers of silver, Congress passed the Bland-Allison Act in 1878. The Act required the U.S. government to purchase large quantities of silver and turn it into silver dollars. Thus, the dollar denomination was restored once again in the form of the Morgan dollar. Named after its designer, George T. Morgan, the Morgan dollar is one of the most popular of all American coins. It's large size, abundant supply, and pleasing appearance make it both affordable and desirable.
Historical notes:

Libertas (Latin for "liberty") is the Roman goddess and personification of liberty.

(To the right) Portrait of Anna Willess Williams (1857-1926), painted by Thomas Eakins (1844-1916) circa 1878. Thomas Eakins suggested to his friend George T. Morgan he use Anna Willess Williams of Philadelphia as a model for Liberty. Williams reluctantly sat for five modelling sessions with Morgan in November 1876 after being convinced to do so by friends. Morgan was impressed with her profile, stating that it was the most perfect he had seen in England or America. The Morgan Dollar bearing her likeness was first struck in 1878. A condition of her sitting for Morgan was that her identity be kept secret, but the subject of the portrait was revealed to be Williams shortly after the dollar was released. Williams was troubled by her newly acquired fame, receiving thousands of letters and visits at both her home and workplace. Williams preferred not to talk about her modelling work with Morgan, dismissing it as an "incident of my youth".

No monetary union existed in Switzerland until the Swiss Republic in 1798, which imposed a single Swiss silver currency, the Swiss franc. Until then the monetary system of St. Gallen was based on the florin (Gulden in German), divided into 60 Kreuzer, themselves divided into 4 Pfennig.
Historical notes:

(To the right) Photograph of the library of the Abbey of St. Gall (Stiftsbibliothek St. Gallen), taken by Peter Thoeny in 2017

The Abbey of St. Gall was inscribed by the United Nations Educational, Scientific and Cultural Organization (UNESCO) on its World Heritage List in 1983. The site is described by UNESCO as "a perfect example of a great Carolingian monastery", which was, "from the 8th century to its secularization in 1805, one of the most important in Europe. Its library is one of the richest and oldest in the world and contains precious manuscripts such as the earliest-known architectural plan drawn on parchment. From 1755 to 1768, the conventual area was rebuilt in Baroque style. The cathedral and the library are the main features of this remarkable architectural complex, reflecting 12 centuries of continuous activity."

The coat of arms of the Abbey of St. Gall was "Or a Bear rampant Sable armed, langued and in his virility Gules". The bear is a symbol that derives from the popular story about St. Gall, an Irish missionary, who built an hermitage where the Abbey of St. Gall was later founded. The story says that once he was travelling in the woods of what is now Switzerland. One evening he was sitting down warming his hands at a fire. A bear emerged from the woods and charged. The holy man rebuked the bear. So awed by his presence the bear stopped its attack and slunk off to the trees. There it gathered firewood before returning to share the heat of the fire with St. Gall. The legend says that for the rest of his days St. Gall was followed around by his companion the bear.

(To the right) Photograph of the library of the Abbey of St. Gall (Stiftsbibliothek St. Gallen), taken by Peter Thoeny in 2017

The Abbey of St. Gall was inscribed by the United Nations Educational, Scientific and Cultural Organization (UNESCO) on its World Heritage List in 1983. The site is described by UNESCO as "a perfect example of a great Carolingian monastery", which was, "from the 8th century to its secularization in 1805, one of the most important in Europe. Its library is one of the richest and oldest in the world and contains precious manuscripts such as the earliest-known architectural plan drawn on parchment. From 1755 to 1768, the conventual area was rebuilt in Baroque style. The cathedral and the library are the main features of this remarkable architectural complex, reflecting 12 centuries of continuous activity."

The coat of arms of the Abbey of St. Gall was "Or a Bear rampant Sable armed, langued and in his virility Gules". The bear is a symbol that derives from the popular story about St. Gall, an Irish missionary, who built an hermitage where the Abbey of St. Gall was later founded. The story says that once he was travelling in the woods of what is now Switzerland. One evening he was sitting down warming his hands at a fire. A bear emerged from the woods and charged. The holy man rebuked the bear. So awed by his presence the bear stopped its attack and slunk off to the trees. There it gathered firewood before returning to share the heat of the fire with St. Gall. The legend says that for the rest of his days St. Gall was followed around by his companion the bear.

Leopold I (born on 9 June 1640 in Vienna; accession on 2 April 1657; died on 5 May 1705)

(To the right) Portrait of Leopold I, painted by Benjamin von Block (1631‚Äì1690) circa 1672

Elected king of Hungary in 1655, he followed suit in 1656 and 1657 in Bohemia and Croatia, respectively. In July 1658, more than a year after the death of his father, the Holy Roman Emperor Ferdinand III, Leopold was elected Holy Roman Emperor at Frankfurt in opposition to the French cardinal Mazarin, who sought to place the imperial crown on the head of Ferdinand Maria, Elector of Bavaria or some other non-Habsburg prince. To conciliate France, which had considerable influence in German affairs thanks to the League of the Rhine, the newly elected Holy Roman Emperor had to promise not to assist Spain, then at war with France. This marked the beginning of a nearly 47-year reign characterised by a lasting rivalry with France and its king, Louis XIV. The dominant personality and power of Louis XIV completely overshadowed Leopold, even to this day, but Leopold was no less a warrior-king given the greater part of his public life was directed towards the arrangement and furtherance of wars.

Varieties:

There are two varieties, for which the letter X has either straight limbs (Class 5a2) or one straight limb and one S-shaped limb (Class 5a3).
Note:

By the middle of Henry III's reign the "Short Cross" coinage in circulation was in a poor state and, in 1247, a new "Long Cross" coinage was ordered with the cross on the reverse being extended to the edge of the coin in an attempt to prevent clipping. The earliest coins (Class 1a) showed the name of neither the mint nor the moneyer. Class 1b includes the name of the mint. From Class 2 onwards all coins show the name of both the mint and the moneyer.

Following publication of the 1908 Brussels Hoard by Churchill and Thomas (2012) the classification of the "Long Cross" coinage was revised. This divided the "Long Cross" coinage into four phases: Phase I (1247, pre-provincial), Phase II (1248-1250, provincial, when sixteen provincial mints, in addition to the main mints of London and Canterbury plus Bury St Edmunds were opened to facilitate the production and distribution of the new coins), Phase III (1250-1272, post-provincial, in which the mint of Durham re-opened, operating intermittently), and Phase IV (1272-1280, posthumous, when the "Long Cross" coinage was continued in the name of Henry III through the first seven years of the reign of Edward I).

In Phases I and II the obverse features the Henry III's head without a sceptre (Classes 1-3) but during Phase III the sceptre was re-introduced (Class 4 onwards). No round hald-pennies ir farthings were struck during the "Long Cross" coinage but cut halves and quarters were used in considerable numbers.

Mint marks features in all coins from Class 1 to 4 and are an important aid to both chronology and identification. Five main varieties of mint mark exist, all in the shape of a star.
Historical notes:

Henry III (born on 1st Octobre 1207 in Winchester; accession on 28 Octobre 1216; died on 16 Novembre 1272)

(To the right) Illustration of the coronation of Henry III, accompanied by a short account of his reign in Anglo-Norman prose, from the recto of the parchment folio f.6 (referred to as f.6r) of the work "Cotton MS Vitellius A XIII", created circa 1280-circa 1300 and conserved at the British Library in London. It is part of a collection of four folios representing the kings of England from Edward the Confessor (r. 1042‚Äì1066) to Edward I (r. 1272‚Äì1307). These folios were extracted in 1939 from a manuscript that was damaged in the fire of Ashburnham House, which was the repository of the Cotton Library, on 23 October 1731, and are now mounted in separate glass frames. In this folio, Henri III is crowned by two accompanying bishops, He is represented as holding in his hand a model of Westminster Abbey, which he enlarged, laying the first stone himself; and caused to be there erected the magnificent monument of Edward the Confessor. It was very common with the illuminators, to make the principal figure holding a model of the abbey, or monastery, they were benefactors to. The author gives a rudimentary description of his reign: "Apres Jon regna Henry le Terz, sun fiz, Ivi aunz ; se fust de ix aunz de age quant fust corone. E en sun tens fust la bataylle de Eveshame, ou fust occys syr Syraund de Munfort, e sun fiz Henry ; e syre Hugh le Despenser, e muz des Barons e des Chevalers de Engletere ‚Äì Puis morust cyl Henry le Roy, e gyst a Westminster.", which can be translated in English as "After John, reigned Henry the Third, his son, 56 years; he was nine years of age when he was crowned. In his time was the battle of Evesham, where was slain Sir Simon de Mountfort, and his son Henry; and Sir Hugh le Despenser, and many barons and knights of England. ‚Äì After died this Henry the king, and lies [buried] at Westminster.‚Äù

Henry III, King John's son, was only nine when he became king. By 1227, when he assumed power from his regent, order had been restored, based on his acceptance of Magna Carta. However, the king's failed campaigns in France (1230 and 1242), his choice of friends and advisers, together with the cost of his scheme to make one of his younger sons, Edmund, King of Sicily and help the Pope against the Holy Roman Emperor, led to further disputes with the barons and united opposition in Church and State.

Although Henry was extravagant and his tax demands were resented, the king's accounts show a list of many charitable donations and payments for building works (including the rebuilding of Westminster Abbey which began in 1245).

The Provisions of Oxford (1258) and the Provisions of Westminster (1259) were attempts by the nobles to define common law in the spirit of Magna Carta, control appointments and set up an aristocratic council. Henry tried to defeat them by obtaining papal absolution from his oaths, and enlisting the help of King Louis IX (saint Louis) of France. Henry renounced the Provisions in 1262 and war broke out. The barons, under their leader, Simon de Montfort, were initially successful and even captured Henry. However, Henry escaped, joined forces with the lords of the Marches (on the Welsh border), and finally defeated and killed de Montfort at the Battle of Evesham in 1265. Royal authority was restored by the Statute of Marlborough (1267), in which the king also promised to uphold Magna Carta and some of the Provisions of Westminster.

Henry died in 1272 and his tomb is in Westminster Abbey, in front of the Church's High Altar, the former resting place of Edward the Confessor, who his son Edward I was named after.

Note:

In 274, Aurelian undertook an important reform of the coinage which, under his immediate predecessors, had suffered a catastrophic decline. Three years earlier his eradication of abuses in the practices of the Rome mint had led to a revolt of the moneyers which seems to have brought about the temporary closure of the facility. Between 271 and 274 an interim improvement in the quality of the billon coinage saw a small increase in the average weight of the coins and an insistence on the employment of higher standards in their production. Finally, the full reform of 274 saw an increase in the average weight of the antoninianus to about 3.85 g and an improvement in its silver content to 4.5%. The reformed antoninianus also normally bore the mark of value XXI (or KA in Greek numerals at some mints), which is usually taken to mean that the coin was worth 1/20th of its equivalent weight in pure silver. Some scholars have preferred to assign the somewhat awkward name of "aurelianianus" (or "Aurelianus") to the reformed antoninianus. However, there seems no pressing need to depart from the traditional nomenclature simply because Aurelian had enacted a much-needed reform of a long-established denomination that had undergone a series of debasements over the preceding decades. Other denominations featured in Aurelian's reformed coinage were a smaller billon piece with laureate portrait, probably a denarius; a laureate bronze coin, usually regarded as a sestertius of reduced weight; and a larger radiate bronze, probably a reduced double sestertius.

With the restoration of the Empire to its full territorial extent during Aurelian‚Äôs reign, the system of mints was inevitably expanded. In Italy Rome was initially augmented by Milan, but in 274 the mint establishment was transferred to nearby Ticinum. With the conquest of the Gallic Empire in the same year the rebel mints of Cologne and Mainz (or Trier) were closed and were replaced by Lugdunum, which had not been active since the rebellion of Clodius Albinus eight decades before. In the militarily important region of the Balkans the products of Siscia were augmented by a new establishment at Serdica, noted for its idiosyncratic obverse legends, and also by a mint the precise location of which has yet to be determined. Cyzicus continued to be active in Asia Minor while Antioch, liberated from the control of the Palmyrene Empire in 272, resumed its role as the major producer of currency in the eastern Mediterranean area. It was assisted by a new mint opened by Aurelian about 273 and attributed to the Phoenician city of Tripolis.

The vast antoninianus coinage of Probus is of exceptional interest and the system of mint marks employed is of considerable complexity. Another interesting feature of Probus' coinage, which provided a limited foretaste of future developments under the Tetrarchy, was the expansion of mint marks in some issues to include the initial letter or letters of the mint name. But perhaps the most noteworthy characteristic, on antoniniani (and aurei), was the dramatic proliferation of obverse bust varieties, unmatched by the coinage of any other reign, to include numerous left-facing military types and others showing the emperor in an elaborate imperial mantle and holding an eagle-tipped sceptre. Such busts had appeared under previous emperors and were to recur occasionally in succeeding reigns, but they were always limited issues, which are rare today. Under Probus, however, they formed part of the regular output of many of the mints.
Historical notes:

(Marcus Aurelius) Probus (born circa 232 in Sirmium; proclaimed emperor in 276; died in autumn 282 near Sirmium)

(To the right) Bust of Probus, sculpted by an unknown artist between 276 and 282, displayed in the Hall of the Emperors of the Palazzo Nuovo in Rome

Probus, the son of a military tribune, had a brilliant military career spanning the reigns of Valerian (253-260) to Tacitus (275-276) and attained the highest eminence as a general. It was under the reign of Aurelian (270-275) that he became one of the premier general of the Empire. Under the reign Tacitus he received the supreme command in the East, a powerful and highly prestigious posting. Soon after the death of Tacitus, Probus was proclaimed emperor by his troops. Probus had little difficulty in overcoming the feeble opposition of Florianus, the younger half-brother of the late-emperor Tacitus, who had assumed the imperial power, and his election was confirmed by the Senate in 276.

Probus then proceeded to repel a series of barbarian threats to the imperial frontiers: Alamannic and Frankish invaders were expelled across the Rhine frontier; the Burgundians and Vandals were defeated in Raetia; the Sarmatians and the Scythians were crushed in Thrace; the Pamphylian looters and pirates were eliminated in Asia Minor; the Blemmyes invasions were stopped in Africa. By 280 he was able to turn his attention to the East where his primary objective was to confront the Sasanid Persians. However, he had to face the usurpations of Saturninus, Bonosus and Proculus. In the meantime, he signed a peace treaty with the Persian shahanshah Bahram II. Having triumphed over all his opponents, Probus returned to Rome in 281 and celebrated his victories. Before preparing a new expedition against the Sasanid Persians, he fell victim to the wrath of his own soldiers while supervising a land reclamation project near Sirmium in 282.

Varieties:

There are two varieties, for which the hippopotamus looks down (RSC4#63 and Cohen5#63) or up (RSC4#64 and Cohen5#64).
Note:

In 215 Caracalla reformed the monetary system to finance ever increasing imperial expenditures by introducing the antoninianus, named after Caracalla's official name "Antoninus". The newly introduced antoninianus had the same silver purity as the denarius (51.5%) but weighed 5.1 g, compared to 3.23 g for the denarius. Although its silver content was only equivalent to about one and a half denarii, the antoninianus was apparently officially tariffed as the equivalent of two. For this reason, it is sometimes referred to as "double-denarius". This new coin always shows the emperor wearing the radiate crown instead of the laurel wreath of the denarius. In the case of empresses, the coin is distinguished by the addition of a crescent placed beneath the bust. It was initially mostly silver, but was slowly debased, following the trend of the debasement of silver during the Severan dynasty (193-235), to bronze with a minimal silver content (see Pense, 1992). Elagabalus demonetised the antoninianus in 219 in favour of the denarius. The antoninianus was re-introduced in 238 by Gordian III who, in turn, saw to it intentionally or otherwise that the denarius would be phased out for good.

During the years 247 and 248 the "Saeculares" coinage struck to commemorate the 1000th anniversary of the foundation of Rome included the following reverse inscriptions: "SAECLARES AVGG" (the age of the Two Emperors), "SAECVLVM NOVVM" (the new age) and "MILIARIVM SAECVLVM" (the millenia). The "Saeculares" coinage is divided into two reverse series: the reverse animal series showing wild animals (with the inscription "SAECLARES AVGG") and the reverse architectural series illustrating various temples (with the inscription "SAECVLVM NOVVM") and cippus (columns) (with the inscription "MILIARIVM SAECVLVM"). The reverse animal series shows at least eight different wild animals: a lion, a she-wolf (suckling the twins Romulus and Remus), a gazelle, a European elk (moose), a hippopotamus, a stag, a goat (or hind), and an antelope. Struck in a wide range of denominations the series included the gold aureus, the silver antoninianius and quinarius, and the bronze sestertius, dupondius and as. Included also are issues of Otacilia Severa, wife of Philip (I) the Arab, and Philip (Il) the Younger, their son who had been raised to the rank of Augustus in 247.

The "Ludi Saeculares" (Secular Games) organised for the 1000th anniversary of the foundation of Rome was highlighted by a splendid array of wild animals. These wild animals had originally been collected for the intended triumph of Gordian III in the Persian war, which saw his death and the associated proclamation of Philip (I) the Arab as emperor in 244. The animals were used by Philip (I) the Arab in magnificent displays and (slaughter) games put on in the key venues, like the Circus Maximus and the Colosseum. This large collection of exotic wild beasts included several specimens of antelope, elephant, European elk (moose), gazelle, giraffe, goat, hippopotamus, wild horse, hyena, leopard, lion, rhinoceros, stag, tiger and wild ass. It was these animals, collected for Gordian III, which, no doubt, inspired the many animal reverse types found on the "Saeculares" coinage struck to commemorate the 1000th anniversary of the foundation of Rome. Besides, the she-wolf (suckling the twins Romulus and Remus) would have been recognised as the symbol of Rome rather than as a reminder of the games' arena.

The mint at Rome striking the "Saeculares" coinage was apparently divided into six officinae (workshops), which are of much interest in that the eight animals illustrated were divided among them with Officina I striking only the lion, II the lion and the she-wolf, III the gazelle and the European elk (moose), IV the hippopotamus, V the stag, and VI the goat (or hind) and the antelope. Also, generally speaking, the six officinae were divided as to the imperial personages shown on the obverse; Officinae I , II, V and VI minted for Philip (I) the Arab, Officina III minted for Philip (II) the Younger, and Officina IV minted for Otacilia Severa.
Historical notes:

(Marcia) Otacilia Severa (born circa 220; given the title of Augusta in 244; died in or after 249)

(To the right) Bust of Otacilia Severa, sculpted by an unknown artist between 244 and 249, displayed in the Hall of the Machines of the Centrale Montemartini in Rome

Marcia Otacilia Severa married Philip (I) the Arab in 234, though very little is known of the biographical details of her life. She may have been of noble Roman birth, as suggested by her name, though if so we can only speculate on how she came to mary the son of an Arab chieftain. She gave birth to their only son, Philip (II) the Younger, about 237 (that is about seven years before her husband's accession as Emperor) and was accorded the title of Augusta early in the reign in 244. She enjoyed a generous share of the imperial coinage, with one of the six officinae of the Rome mint being assigned to the production of issues in her name. The Antioch mint also struck antoniniani for her, though these are by no means common. Otacilia's ultimate fate is shrouded in mystery. She was in Rome when her husband was defeated and killed at the Battle of Verona in 249 following a rebellion led by his successor, Decius. However, it is unclear whether she was murdered as their son Philip (II) the Younger by the Praetorians shortly after the death of her husband or merely permitted to live a private life in obscurity.

Varieties:

There are two varieties, for which the cornucopia is below the seat (RIC3#35) or appears to hold the statuette of Specs further away behind the seat (RIC3#40).
Note:

Following the trend of the debasement of silver introduced by Nero in 64, Marcus Aurelius further devalued the denarius upon his accession in 161 to pay for constant wars and increased expenses. The silver content of the denarius was decreased from 83.5% to 79%, the silver weight dropping from 2.68 g to 2.57 g. In 168, he revalued the denarius, increasing its silver content from 79% to 82%, the silver weight increasing from 2.57 g to 2.67 g. However, two years later he reverted to the previous values because of the military crises facing the empire.

Because of the unprecedented length of time during which Marcus Aurelius bore the junior Imperial rank of Caesar (22 years) his coinage under Antoninus is abundant in the full range of denominations. During his own reign Marcus struck additional issues in the names of his wife Faustina Junior, his daughter Lacilla, his co-emperors Lucius Verus and, later, Commodus, and posthumous issues for Divus Antoninus (161), Divus Verus (169) and Diva Faustina (176). At the very end of the reign (from 178) coins were struck for Crispina, wife of the young co-emperor Commodus. However, as her coinage continued into the sole reign of her husband all issues are normally associated with Commodus.
Historical notes:

Marcus Aurelius (Antoninus) (born on 26 April 121 in Rome; accession on 7 March 161; died on 17 March 180)

(To the right) Busts of the Co-emperors Marcus Aurelius and Lucius Verus, sculpted by an unknown artist between 161 and 170, displayed in Room 70 of the British Museum in London

Originally named Marcus Annius Catilius Severus, the future Emperor Marcus Aurelius Antoninus was born in Rome in 121 and was the nephew of the Empress Faustina Senior, wife of Antoninus Pius (138-161). Following the early death of his father Marcus Annius Verus, brother of the Empress Faustina Senior, in 124, he was brought up in the household of their father and his grandfather, also named Marcus Annius Verus, who had an illustrious public career and was three times consul, a rare distinction for someone outside the Imperial family. The Emperor Hadrian (117-138), who adopted Antoninus Pius, recognised the fine qualities of the youth and arranged for his betrothal to the daughter of Aelius Caesar, who he had also adopted. Later following the death of Aelius Caesar in 138, Hadrian stipulated that the new Caesar, Antoninus Pius, should adopt Marcus Aurelius together with Aelius Caesar's son, Lucius Verus. On the accession of Antoninus Pius in the summer of 138 Marcus Aurelius' career was rapidly advanced and he was even promoted to the rank of Caesar in 139, a clear indication that the new emperor regarded him, rather than Lucius Verus, as the heir to the Imperial throne. The annulment of Marcus Aurelius' betrothal to the daughter of Aelius Caesar provided additional confirmation of Antoninus Pius' intentions, as the young man was now betrothed to the emperor's daughter, Faustina the Younger. In 145 the couple were married and two years later the 26-year-old Caesar was granted the tribunician power. Antoninus Pius survived to the age of 74 and Marcus Aurelius was already in his fortieth year by the time he ascended the throne at his death in 161.

Loyal to the memory of the Emperor Hadrian, one of his first acts as emperor was to associate the younger Lucius Verus with him as co-ruler, thus inaugurating the first joint reign in almost two hundred years of Imperial history. Unfortunately, the experiment was not a resounding success. Lucius Verus proved to be weak and addicted to the pursuit of pleasure and was thus more of a liability than an asset in the demanding task of governing a vast Empire. This merely confirmed Antoninus Pius' earlier judgement and the sudden and untimely death of Lucius Verus in 169 must have come as a considerable relief to Marcus Aurelius. Although gentle and philosophical by nature, Marcus Aurelius was fated to spend long periods of his reign enduring the harsh conditions of military camp life. Throughout the 170s he undertook arduous campaigns on the Rhine and Danube frontiers, where there was much unrest amongst the barbarian tribes, and it was during this trying time that he wrote his celebrated "Meditations" based on the principles of Stoic philosophy. Following the abortive revolt in the East of the general Avidius Cassius in 175, Marcus Aurelius decided to advance his young son Commodus to Imperial status, first as Caesar (175) and then as Augustus and co-emperor late in the following year. Worn out by the cares of state Marcus Aurelius died at the age of 59 in 180 and was succeeded by the 19-year-old co-emperor Commodus. He was immediately deified, an extensive posthumous coinage being issued in his honour. A careful, generous and conscientious autocrat, it has been written of Marcus Aurelius that "in the evening of Rome's greatness her ruler fittingly personified the virtues that had been her glory".

Varieties:

There are three varieties, for which the bull is butting right and Augustus' head is facing right (RCV1#1610, RSC1#137 and Cohen1#137) or left (RSC1#139 and Cohen1#139) and the bull is butting left and Augustus' head is facing right (RSC1#141 and Cohen1#141).
Note:

Augustus reformed the monetary system between 23 and 15 BC in order to solve the disorder that had been created in Rome's monetary production. Monetary issues were very numerous, thanks to the spoils that the "princeps" had brought back from the new province of Egypt and the Spanish mines, so much so that it resulted in an excess of money, leading to a cut in interest rates and an increase in the value of landed property. The reform brought an end to emergency issues, minted by mobile mints following the various generals of the Roman Empire, as had been the case during the civil wars. The weight of the silver denarius ‚Äì which remained the reference currency ‚Äì was restored following a considerable drop during the civil wars. It was now valued 84 to the pound and the gold aureus was valued at 25 denarii and 41 to the pound. The weight of the Roman pound is normally reckoned at 327.45 g, giving the denarius and the aureus theoretical weights in modern terms of 3.90 g and 7.99 g, respectively. Denominations below the denarius were more comprehensively reformed. New ratios were fixed among the currencies: the sestertius was now minted from about an ounce of orichalcum, an alloy of copper and zinc, rather than silver, and fixed at a quarter of a denarius. The dupondius, formerly a two-pound bronze coin, was now orichalcum, valued at half a sestertius and weighing half as much. The half-ounce as, worth half a dupondius, the semis, worth half an as, and the quadrans, worth half a semis, were the pure copper coins. The Augustan currency system was to set the pattern for the Roman Imperial coinage for the first two and a half centuries of its history.

The minting of gold and silver coins was brought under under Augustus' personal control and used the new mint of Lugdunum, while the minting of coins from copper and its alloys was left under senatorial control and used the mint of Rome. The latter coins generally bore the inscription "SC" (Senatus Consultum; "by Decree of the Senate"). The reform also marked the definitive presence of the portrait of a living person on coins.
Historical notes:

(Gaius Julius Caesar) Augustus (born on 23 September 63 BC in Rome; proclaimed emperor on 16 January 27 BC; died on 19 August AD 14 in Nola)

(To the right) Bust of Augustus, sculpted by an unknown artist between 27 BC and AD 14, displayed in the Hall of the Emperors of the Palazzo Nuovo in Rome

Through the gradual elimination of all his political rivals over the period 44-30 BC, and the constitutional reorganisations of the early years of his reign, the emperor Augustus had, with the utmost subtlety guided the transition of the Roman government from a republic to a monarchy. The changes enacted in the summer of 23 BC put the finishing touches to this process. In this year he resigned the consulship, which he had held continuously since 31 BC, receiving in its place proconsular imperium ever all of Rome's territories. At the same time he obtained a lifetime grant of the tribunician power ("tribunicia potestas") thereby acquiring the absolute right of veto. These developments were of immense importance as the gave Augustus, and all of his successors, complete control of the state. Despite the outward appearance of constitutionality in the form of his authority Augustus had effected a revolution in the government of the Roman Empire. What was even more remarkable was that he had accomplished the transformation almost imperceptibly, so awed were the Roman people with the personal "auctoritas" of their leader, now generally referred to as the "princeps". Under his enlightened administration the Roman Empire prospered and stability was restored to all aspects of life, from the revival of agriculture in war-torn Italy to the complete reform of the state's finances and money.

Despite his delicate constitution and frequent bouts of ill-health Augustus lived for seventy-seven years. In the latter part of his life he became increasingly concerned over the question of the imperial succession, especially as he had begun to outlive not only his contemporaries but also some of the younger members of the imperial family. His old friend Agrippa expired suddenly and unexpectedly in 12 BC and even Agrippa's sons, Gaius and Lucius Caesars, grandsons of Augustus, died tragically in AD 4 and AD 2 respectively. Augustus was left with little choice but to designate Tiberius, the empress Livia's son by an earlier marriage, as heir to the throne. His relationship with Tiberius had never been good and this unfortunate turn of events doubtless embittered the emperor's final years. He died on 19 August AD 14 in the Campanian town of Nola. His legacy was a peaceful and secure state free from the strife that had characterised the final decades of the Senate's rule. It was small wonder than when this man passed away there was universal grieving and a strong desire to honour his memory and commemorate his enormous achievements, which had affected the lives of every citizen.

Note:

In 1897 Peru finally joined the decades-old worldwide trend of switching from a bimetallic currency standard to a single metal gold standard. The new Peruvian pound (Libra Peruana) was established as a gold coin weighing 7.9881 grams of 917‚Ä∞ purity (0.2355 troy ounces of pure gold). Sol-denominated coins continued to circulate and were valued as fractions of a Libra, at 10 Sols to 1 Libra by fiat, but the non-gold coins were token coinage and were legal tender only up to a total of just 100 sols.

The first gold 1 Libra coins appeared in 1898. The first ¬Ω Libra coin followed in 1902, and the first ‚Öï Libra coin followed in 1906. The worldwide financial crisis that began in 1929 caused instability in gold bullion prices and this instability disrupted the monetary systems of gold-standard countries, including Peru, and issuance of the gold Libra coins for circulation halted in 1930. Issuance of the ‚Öï Libra and ¬Ω Libra coins resumed in 1953, and the 1 Libra coin reappeared in 1959 but the post-1930 issues are generally considered bullion issues and were not intended for standard circulation. Issuance of all denominations of gold Libra coins finally ceased in 1969.
Historical notes:

Manco C√°pac ("Manqu Qhapaq" in Quechua) (circa‚Äâ1200-circa 1230)

(To the right) Portrait of Manco C√°pac, attributed to the School of Cusco and painted in the 18th century

Manco C√°pac was, according to some historians, the first sovereign and founder of the Inca civilisation in Cusco, possibly in the early 13th century. He is the main figure of Inca mythology, his figure being mentioned in several chronicles, but his actual existence remains uncertain.

Note:

The gold currency sytem during the Tokugawa shogunate was based on the ry≈ç, divided into four bu, themselves divided into four shu. They all bear the mark of (Got≈ç) Mitsutsugu.

Mitsutsugu is the name given by the Got≈ç family to their clerk (tedai, Êâã‰ª£) Hashimoto Sh≈çzabur≈ç (Ê©ãÊú¨Â∫Ñ‰∏âÈÉé) (1571-1625) at the time of Tokuj≈ç Got≈ç (1549-1631). In 1592 the head of the Tokugawa clan and future sh≈çgun Tokugawa Ieyasu (1603-1605), whose power was growing, consulted Tokuj≈ç Got≈ç, who was serving in the gold mint in Kyoto for the head of the Toyotomi clan and Chancellor of the Realm (Daij≈ç Daijin) Toyotomi Hideyoshi (1537-1598), to obtain the service of some noted expert whom he might take with him to establish a gold mint in his own domain in Edo. However, none cared to leave Kyoto to go to the east. Whereupon there appeared Hashimoto Sh≈çzabur≈ç, who of his accord volunteered to go, and was taken into service by Tokugawa Ieyasu. With this important appointment he was allowed to bear the family name Got≈ç and their 3-5-3 kiri (paulownia) crest (consisting of three leaves of the paulownia surmounted by three budding stems with an inflorescence of 3‚Äì5‚Äì3 flowers) and was given the name Mitsutsugu. Toyotomi Hideyoshi had introduced the Oban (Â§ßÂà§, "large size"), which was was worth ten ry≈ç. Mitsutsugu divided the Oban into 10 Koban (Â∞èÂà§, literally "small size"), which were then worth one ry≈ç, a more practical value for commercial transactions. Like the Oban, the Koban had an oval shape. These Koban were stamped, as guarantee of the quality of the gold, with a paulownia seal (Ê°êÁ¥ã, kirimon), consisting of three leaves of the paulownia surmounted by three budding stems. In 1599, the Koban was further divided into four Buban. In contrast to the Koban (and the Oban), the Buban had a rectangular shape. These Buban were also stamped with the paulownia seal. This regional issue from Edo blossomed during the Tokugawa shogunate. The rectangle-shaped coins branched into three denominations: ry≈ç, bu and shu. They eventually spilled over into silver pieces denominated in the second half of the 18th century.

Their circulation was stopped around Meiji 7 (1874) when the gold and silver coins of the Edo period lost their status of legal tender.
Historical notes:

(To the right) Portrait of Tokugawa Iesada, attributed to the Kan≈ç School and painted in the 19th century

Tokugawa Iesada (1824-1858) was the 13th sh≈çgun of the Tokugawa shogunate from 1853 to 1858. He was physically weak and was therefore considered by later historians to have been unfit to be sh≈çgun. His reign saw the beginning of the Bakumatsu period (1853-1868) that led to the end of the Tokugawa shogunate.

Varieties:

There are three varieties, for which the three co-emperor stepsons of Romanos IV are standing on footstools set on a single dais represented as a dotted exergual band (BCV#1860 and DOC3_2#2) or without the single dais (BCV#1859 and DOC3_2#1) or without the footstools (BCV#1861).
Note:

The twin pivots of the Byzantine monetary system in the 5th and 6th centuries had been the gold solidus or nomisma and the copper nummus. The gold solidus was a coin struck 72 to the Roman pound and consequently weighing, in Greco-Roman units, 24 siliquae or carats. The weight of the Roman pound is normally reckoned at 327.45 g, giving the solidus a theoretical weight in modern terms of 4.55 g. The nummus, sometimes called by contemporaries a denarius, was a small coin of varying weight, usually about 1 g or a little less. It has then been reckoned as 1/7200 of the solidus, but its value in fact varied and seems frequently to have fallen to a much lower figure. The gap between the solidus and the nummus was bridged in part by real coins (the gold semissis and tremissis, fractions of the solidus, and the copper pentanummium, decanummium, half follis, and follis, multiples of the nummus), in part by a money of account, the keration, and in part by the miliaresion, which was sometimes one and sometimes the other.

Treating them in descending order of value, the semissis and tremissis require no discussion. They were actual coins, worth one-half and one-third of a solidus, respectively. Miliaresion was a Greek loan word from the Latin "miliarense", a term applied to a silver coin current in the 4th century from the period of the Tetrarchy onward. In the 6th century, when no silver coinage was being struck for regular circulation in the East, it had become the generic name for any silver coin at all, being applied indifferently to the Roman denarius and to the Persian dirham. When the hexagram was created by the Byzantine emperor Heraclius (r. 610‚Äì641) this was known as the silver coin of six grammata, and when the hexagram ceased to be struck the term remained available for application to whatever silver coin would take its place.

The keration was not a coin, but primarily a unit of weight, 1/1728 of the Roman pound, 1/144 of the Roman ounce, or about 0.189 g in modern terms. Since the solidus was one-sixth of a Roman ounce, the solidus weighed 24 keratia. Consequently, as a money of account, the keration was 1/24 of the Roman solidus, a relationship that lasted throughout the whole of the Byzantine Empire's history. Keratia are omnipresent in Byzantine monetary reckonings, but they are always notional. A debt expressed in solidi, keratia, and fractions of keratia would be paid in solidi, miliaresia, folles, and such fractions of these as were in circulation at the time.

The follis, sometimes called the obol, reintroduced in 498 with the monetary reform of the Byzantine emperor Anastasius I Dicorus (r. 491-518), was a copper coin worth forty nummi. Various fractions were struck during the 6th and 7th centuries, the half follis, decanummium, and pentanummium being the most important. In the mid 6th century the follis was reckoned 180 to the solidus. How the relationships between the solidus, keration, follis and nummus were affected by the "debasement" of the follis in the later 6th and 7th centuries ‚Äì the gradual reduction of its weight to less than one-sixth of what it had been in the peak years of Justinian I's reign (527-565) ‚Äì is a question to which there is not yet any answer. The nummus disappeared completely as a coin, but since the follis and its fractions still bore marks of value expressed as multiples of nummi, these were presumably still thought of as the lowest units in the monetary system.

This monetary system was not as unified in the three and a half centuries between the accession of the Byzantine emperor Leo III (r. 717-741) in 717, marking the end of the "Twenty Years' Anarchy" (695-717), and the monetary reform of the Byzantine emperor Alexios I Komnenos (r. 1081-1118) in 1092, following his accession in 1081, marking the end of a prolonged period of civil war resulting from the defeat to the Seljuk Turks at Manzikert in 1071. The silver miliaresion introduced by Leo III in 720 and continued by his successors provided a certain element of unity nonetheless. The essential features of the monetary system during this period may be summarized as follows:

Gold coinage ‚Äì In the East, the semissis and tremissis ceased to be struck, save as ceremonial coins, toward the middle of the 8th century. Henceforward the only gold coin was the solidus. Its fineness remained virtually unchanged down to the 1030's, when debasement set in, and while its weight seems ot have declined very slightly the coin remained one theoretically struck 72 to the Roman pound. In the reign of Nikephoros II Phokas (r. 963-969), however, there began a series of experiments in light-weight nomismata comparable to the light-weight solidi of the 6th and 7th centuries. Of these the light-weight nomisma of 22 keratia, known as a tetarteron, finally gained the day, so that from the late 10th century down to Alexios I Komnenos' monetary reform of 1092 one has to distinguish between the full-weight nomisma histamenon and the light-weight nomisma tetarteron.

Things went differently in the West. The solidus and tremissis continued to be struck in mainland Italy as long as the mints of Ravenna and Rome remained in Byzantine hands, i.e., to the mid and late 8th century, respectively. In Sicily they continued down to the reign of Basil I (r. 867-886), and there was even a revival of the use of the semissis in the early 9th century. The weight standard of the western gold coins, however, was substantially less than that of their eastern counterparts, and both on the mainland and in Sicily there was a serious reduction in fineness during the last decades of Byzantine rule. The fate of the fractional gold was related inversely to the presence or absence of a silver coinage, since the miliaresion flourished in the East but was not adopted in the West.

Silver coinage ‚Äì The history of the silver coinage is that of a new miliaresion, a coin much lighter than the hexagram of the 7th century, which was introduced by Leo III in 720. It lasted, with remarkably few changes in external appearance, down to Alexios I Komnenos' monetary reform of 1092. The weight, however, was several times altered, and in its final phase it was badly debased in both weight and fineness. Fractions were struck only under Leo III, of whose half miliaresion specimens are known, and in the middle of the 11th century. Over much of the period the miliaresion was valued as a double keration, i.e., as a coin worth 1/12 of a solidus, so that sums of money are as commonly expressed in terms of nomismata and miliaresia as of nomismata and keratia. But when this ratio became fixed, or how the value of the actual coins changed, in relation to the debased nomisma, during the last decades of their existence, are not known exactly.

Copper Coinage ‚Äì The evolution of the copper resembled that of the gold in that both underwent a process of drastic simplification and differed from it in that the weight of the follis altered greatly over the centuries.
Historical notes:

Romanos IV (Diogenes) (born circa 1030; crown emperor on 1st January 1068; died in 1072)

(To the right) Photograph of the Grand √áamlƒ±ca Mosque in Istanbul, Turkey, taken by an anonym in 2021. The landmark complex was completed in 2019. The mosque has six minarets, which represent the six articles of Islamic faith (iman). Four of the minarets, centred around the central dome, feature three balconies that stand 107.1-metre high in recognition of the victory of the Seljuk Turks at Manzikert in 1071. The other two minarets, on the outer ends of the mosque, feature two balconies and are 90-metre high.

The final act of Constantine X (Doukas) before he died in 1067 was to demand that only his two sons he had elevated to co-emperors, Michael and Konstantios, succeed him, thereby forcing his wife Eudokia (Makrembolitissa) to take a vow not to remarry. Michael was about 17-year old while Konstantios was about 7-year old at the time. Since they were too young to rule, Eudokia ruled as regent until 1st January 1068, when she married Romanos IV (Diogenes) and crowned him emperor. Romanos IV was now the senior emperor and guardian of his stepsons Michael, Konstantios, and Andronikos who he also elevated to co-emperor. However, his accession to the throne had antagonised not only the Doukas family, in particular Constantine X (Doukas)'s brother John Doukas who led the opposition of the palace officials to Romanos IV (Diogenes) authority, but also the Varangian Guard, who openly expressed their discontent at the marriage of Eudokia. Romanos IV (Diogenes) therefore decided that he could only exercise his authority by placing himself at the head of the army in the field, thereby focusing the whole government's attention on the war against the Seljuk Turks.

By 1067, the Seljuk Turks had been making incursions at will across the eastern border of the Byzantine Empire. In 1068, the new emperor immediately set about trying to defend Asia Minor against the advance of the Seljuk Turks. He was confident of Byzantine superiority on the field of battle, looking on the Seljuk Turks as little more than hordes of robbers who would melt away at the first encounter. He did not take into account the degraded state of the Byzantine army, which had suffered years of neglect from his predecessors, in particular Constantine X (Doukas). With an army composed mainly of ill-disciplined, disorganised, and uncoordinated foreign mercenaries he achieved some initial success, but at the famous battle of Mantzikert in 1071 the Seljuk Turks won a devastating victory and Romanus IV (Diogenes) himself was taken prisoner. The opposition party in Constantinople took the opportunity to depose the unfortunate emperor and re-establish Eudokia on the throne together with her eldest son Michael VII (Dukas).

There is a large variety in the types and styles of the issues of individual caliphs. The earlier caliphs settled on one or two main types, but varieties made their appearance, especially from mints far from the capital. In the middle years of the FƒÅ·π≠imid Caliphate, new types were introduced periodically, but there was often an overlap and several types might have been produced in the same mint in the same years. In Sicily, which seems to have been a world of its own, multiple types were struck, seemingly in a single mint, in any given year. This may mean that a single mint, perhaps that at Palemo, operated a number of workshops and the die makers of each workshop were able to create the dies in an independent manner, at least in deciding the design of the coin.
Note:

The early centre of the FƒÅ·π≠imid Caliphate, which controlled, at various times, much of the Islamic world from Morocco to Syria, was situated in that part of North Africa which had been ruled by the Aghlabid Emirate (800-909). Therefore, it was natural that the earliest Fatimid coinage resembled that of the Aghlabids in style, fabric and appearance. Only the date, mint and inscriptions differentiated the coins of the FƒÅ·π≠imids from the issues of their predecessors. It is likely that Aghlabid and FƒÅ·π≠imid coins circulated together during the first decades of the new regime in Ifriqƒ´yah and Sicily, that is, until enough of the older coinage had been melted and reminted in the FƒÅ·π≠imid model that the latter would predominate in circulation.

The coinage issues of individual caliphs are surprisingly quite varied and distinctively different given that the FƒÅ·π≠imid Caliphate's rule lasted for 260-odd years. The coins of the FƒÅ·π≠imids fall into two main categories: the gold dinar and its fractions and the silver dirham and its fractions and multiples. Within these two categories, the dinar itself and the half dirham are the two denominations that have survived to the present day in rather large quantities. Some denominations were peculiar to certain geographical areas, whereas others were common to all parts of the FƒÅ·π≠imid realm. The dinar was the standard gold coin in use throughout the Fatimid Empire, except in Sicily, where the quarter dinar predominated. The half dirham, common throughout North Africa and Egypt, was almost unknown in Sicily, which had a variety of silver fractions in use throughout the island. The half dirham was also not common in Syria and Palestine, but full dirhams and even silver coins of approximately two dirhams weight are not unknown, although one hesitates to call the latter "double dirhams." Billon coinage was produced by a number of Palestinian mints and are known in a wide range of weights, but were probably all generically called dishams. The silver issues of the later FƒÅ·π≠imid caliphs, almost exclusively struck in Egypt were the small, irregularly-shaped black dirhams, the production of which was continued by the Ayy≈´bid Sultanate of Egypt (1171-1260). A very few copper or bronze coins have been encountered. Some of these exhibit traces of a silver wash, which indicated that they were not considered to be copper coins as such, but rather small change in the dirham series.

FƒÅ·π≠imid coinage, and particularly gold coinage, played an important part in the economy of North Africa and the Middle East during the period of the FƒÅ·π≠imid Caliphate. Indeed, the FƒÅ·π≠imid dinar had a wider impact throughout the Mediterranean world because of the reputation the regime's coinage carried with it far beyond the borders of the empire. FƒÅ·π≠imid gold coins were struck to a high standard of fineness and this standard was generally maintained during most of the time the FƒÅ·π≠imid Caliphate was in power. In the Syrian and Palestinian territories, the dinar was frequently struck to a lighter standard, especially in the later years of the FƒÅ·π≠imid Caliphate.
Historical notes:

al-·∫íƒÅhir (li-I'zƒÅz Dƒ´n AllƒÅh) (ÿßŸÑÿ∏ÿßŸáÿ± ŸÑÿ•ÿπÿ≤ÿßÿ≤ ÿØŸäŸÜ ÿßŸÑŸÑŸá) (born on 10 RamadƒÅn AH 395 (20 June 1005); proclaimed caliph on 10 Dh≈´ al-Hijjah AH 411 (27 March 1021); died on 15 Sha'bƒÅn AH 427 (13 June 1036))

(To the right) Photograph of the al-Azhar Mosque in Cairo, Egypt, taken by an anonym in 2018. The FƒÅ·π≠imid Caliphate conquered Egypt and founded Cairo (al-QƒÅhira) in AH 358 (969). The mosque was established in AH 361 (972) and the capital of the FƒÅ·π≠imid Caliphate moved from al-Man·π£≈´rƒ´yah to Cairo in AH 362 (972-973).

Some time passed after the disappearance of his predecessor al-·∏§ƒÅkim on 27 ShawwƒÅl AH 411 (13 February 1021) before al-·∫íƒÅhir was proclaimed caliph on 10 Dh≈´ al-Hijjah AH 411 (27 March 1021). The FƒÅ·π≠imid regime was fairly stable during the first few years of al-·∫íƒÅhir's caliphate, but this situation did not last very long. By 414-415 AH (1024-1025), the wasteful extravagances of al-·∏§ƒÅkim's rule began to take a toll on the health of the empire. A financial crisis ensued in which the cash reserves of the treasury were depleted. The army remained unpaid for an overly long period, then riots in Cairo and the provinces broke out, al-·∫íƒÅhir lost control of his government and a ruling clique came to be in charge in the capital. This was followed by first drought and then famine in Egypt during AH 415 (1024-1025).

Despite his political incompetence and a life given over to the pursuit of pleasure, al-·∫íƒÅhir was able to weather the political storm and on 16 JumƒÅdƒÅ II AH 420 (2 July 1029), his heir, Ab≈´ Tamƒ´m Ma'add, who was destined to become the Caliph al-Mustan·π£ir, was born.

Trouble did not long stay away from al-·∫íƒÅhir, however. His military commanders exercised almost independent control and in AH 421 (1030), attacks by the army on the populace in North Africa diminished support for the caliph. A powerful earthquake caused massive destruction in Palestine and al-·∫íƒÅhir spent massive sums to rebuild the walls of Jerusalem during the years AH 421-424 (1030-1033). Famine once again visited North Africa in AH 425 (1034) and in the midst of all these problems, al-·∫íƒÅhir died some two years later, just under thirty-two years of age.

Note:

In 215 Caracalla reformed the monetary system to finance ever increasing imperial expenditures by introducing the antoninianus, named after Caracalla's official name "Antoninus". The newly introduced antoninianus had the same silver purity as the denarius (51.5%) but weighed 5.1 g, compared to 3.23 g for the denarius. Although its silver content was only equivalent to about one and a half denarii, the antoninianus was apparently officially tariffed as the equivalent of two. For this reason, it is sometimes referred to as "double-denarius". This new coin always shows the emperor wearing the radiate crown instead of the laurel wreath of the denarius. In the case of empresses, the coin is distinguished by the addition of a crescent placed beneath the bust. It was initially mostly silver, but was slowly debased, following the trend of the debasement of silver during the Severan dynasty (193-235), to bronze with a minimal silver content (see Pense, 1992). Elagabalus demonetised the antoninianus in 219 in favour of the denarius. The antoninianus was re-introduced in 238 by Gordian III who, in turn, saw to it intentionally or otherwise that the denarius would be phased out for good.

During the years 247 and 248 the "Saeculares" coinage struck to commemorate the 1000th anniversary of the foundation of Rome included the following reverse inscriptions: "SAECLARES AVGG" (the age of the Two Emperors), "SAECVLVM NOVVM" (the new age) and "MILIARIVM SAECVLVM" (the millenia). The "Saeculares" coinage is divided into two reverse series: the reverse animal series showing wild animals (with the inscription "SAECLARES AVGG") and the reverse architectural series illustrating various temples (with the inscription "SAECVLVM NOVVM") and cippus (columns) (with the inscription "MILIARIVM SAECVLVM"). The reverse animal series shows at least eight different wild animals: a lion, a she-wolf (suckling the twins Romulus and Remus), a gazelle, a European elk (moose), a hippopotamus, a stag, a goat (or hind), and an antelope. Struck in a wide range of denominations the series included the gold aureus, the silver antoninianius and quinarius, and the bronze sestertius, dupondius and as. Included also are issues of Otacilia Severa, wife of Philip (I) the Arab, and Philip (Il) the Younger, their son who had been raised to the rank of Augustus in 247.

The "Ludi Saeculares" (Secular Games) organised for the 1000th anniversary of the foundation of Rome was highlighted by a splendid array of wild animals. These wild animals had originally been collected for the intended triumph of Gordian III in the Persian war, which saw his death and the associated proclamation of Philip (I) the Arab as emperor in 244. The animals were used by Philip (I) the Arab in magnificent displays and (slaughter) games put on in the key venues, like the Circus Maximus and the Colosseum. This large collection of exotic wild beasts included several specimens of antelope, elephant, European elk (moose), gazelle, giraffe, goat, hippopotamus, wild horse, hyena, leopard, lion, rhinoceros, stag, tiger and wild ass. It was these animals, collected for Gordian III, which, no doubt, inspired the many animal reverse types found on the "Saeculares" coinage struck to commemorate the 1000th anniversary of the foundation of Rome. Besides, the she-wolf (suckling the twins Romulus and Remus) would have been recognised as the symbol of Rome rather than as a reminder of the games' arena.

The mint at Rome striking the "Saeculares" coinage was apparently divided into six officinae (workshops), which are of much interest in that the eight animals illustrated were divided among them with Officina I striking only the lion, II the lion and the she-wolf, III the gazelle and the European elk (moose), IV the hippopotamus, V the stag, and VI the goat (or hind) and the antelope. Also, generally speaking, the six officinae were divided as to the imperial personages shown on the obverse; Officinae I , II, V and VI minted for Philip (I) the Arab, Officina III minted for Philip (II) the Younger, and Officina IV minted for Otacilia Severa.
Historical notes:

Marcus Julius Severus Philippus (born about 237; given the title of Augustus in 247; died in 249)

(To the right) Bust of Philip (II) the Younger, sculpted by an unknown artist between 247 and 249, displayed in the Mus√©e Saint-Raymond in Toulouse

Philip (II) the Younger, the son of Philip (I) the Arab and Otacilia Severa, was born about 237, that is about seven years before his father's accession as Emperor in 244. On his father's accession as Emperor in 244, he was immediately granted the junior imperial rank of Caesar and this he retained for the following three years. He seems to have accompanied his father during the campaigns of 245-247 against the Quadi and the Carpi on the northern frontier and on their return to Rome to celebrate a triumph the boy was rewarded by advancement to the rank of Augustus and co-emperor with his father. When their regime was threatened two years later in 249 by the revolt of Decius, the father and son marched together against the usurper, only to perish in battle at Verona. An alternative version of the story is that Philip (II) the Younger survived the battle but was brought back to Rome where he was put to death by the Praetorians.

The coinage of Philip (II) the Younger is divided into two main groups, those issued berween 244 and 247, when he bore the title of Caesar, and those issued after his elevation to the rank of Augustus. The former are all from the mint of Rome, but after he became co-emperor with his father he also shared in the issue of antoniniani from the mint of Antioch. During this latter period he used the same obverse legends as his father and differentiation on some issues from the mint of Antioch may sometimes be confusing, though the younger features of his portrait are generally discernible. Like his mother, Philip (II) the Younger was assigned one of the six officinae of the Rome mint to produce issues in his name.

References: RIC2#435; RCV1#2835; Cohen1#674; OCRE#http://numismatics.org/ocre/id/ric.2_1(2).dom.250

Note:

For several centuries before the rise of Rome there had been a tradition of featuring animals, birds, fish and insects (as well as various mythological beasts) on the coinages of many of the Greek city states. Rome inherited this tradition, and although the representation of fauna is less frequent and varied than on coins of the Greek series, they nevertheless form a most appealing group within the Roman coinage.

Crocodile, heifer, bull, wild boar, lion attacking stag, eagle, crab and butterfly, capricorn, Pegasus and Sphinx all appear on the coinage of Augustus, who was the inheritor of the late Republican tradition of great diversity in the selection of coin types. However, during the course of his long reign that tradition was gradually superseded by a more conservative approach to the type content of the new Imperial coinage. Accordingly, the coinages of the later Julio-Claudian emperors feature virtually no representations of animals, other than the elephants drawing the car of Divus Augustus on a sestertius issued by Tiberius, and the eagle appearing on the reverse of a Divus Augustus as.

The Flavian revival of earlier coin types led to a reintroduction of the tradition of animal depiction on the Imperial coinage. A reverse of this period shows a goat being milked by a goat-herd and another has a sow with its young. A few years into Domitian's reign an extraordinary issue of quadrantes were struck, featuring a rhinoceros. Although the coins are undated, their production can be narrowed down between late 83 when he assumed the title Germanicus and 85 when the consular date XI appeared on the quadrantes. The legend "SC" (Senatus Consultum; "by Decree of the Senate") appears traditionally on the reverse so that the rhinoceros must be taken to appear on the obverse. This obverse type is highly unusual in that it breaks with the limited variety of standard obverses that were normally featured on the quadrantes, namely the portrait of the emperor himself or of a divinity. Not only was this issue produced out of series, and just once, it was struck in enormous numbers: of all the issues of the fractions of the as coined during the whole of Domitian's fifteen-year reign, this is the most common today, surviving in twice the number of even the next most common of Domitian's fractions. Hence, the quadrans was intended for the crowd, not for those who would never have handled such small change. Domitian's rhinoceros quadrans was the objective correlative, one might say, of the appearance of his rhinoceros in his innumerable rich and wonderful games and other celebrations. Everyone had the opportunity to see the rhinoceros, everyone could have a reminder of it in a coin that was likely to circulate most widely at the lowest level of the currency. Just as the games were the supreme expression of popular entertainment, the rhinoceros quadrans was their permanent record through the image of the spectacular beast provided by the emperor. Interestingly, Martial in his "Liber de Spectaculis" celebrated twice (in epigrams 11 and 26) the rhinoceros in the Flavian Amphitheatre (the Colosseum). On the basis of the numismatic evidence provided by Domitian's rhinoceros quadrans, Buttrey (2007) argued that Martial‚Äôs text should be dated not (as it nearly universally is) to the reign of Titus but to that of Domitian.

In the 2nd century the Pegasus and the griffin appear on several aes denominations of Hadrian whilst his successor Antoninus Pius struck asses showing an elephant and a sow suckling its young beneath an oak tree, both types probably having reference to the celebrations connected with the 900th anniversary of the foundation of Rome. An attractive representation of a dove appears on an aureus of Antoninus Pius' daughter, the younger Faustina, and elephants occur on an as of Commodus and a denarius of Septimius Severus.

The "king of beasts" is depicted on the coinage of Caracalla, wearing a radiate crown and holding a thunderbolt in its jaws, and several decades later the lion reappears as part of the "Saeculares" series of Philip (I) the Arab. Similarly, the elephant was popular as a coin type during the first half of the 3rd century, appearing on pieces of Caracalla, Geta, and Philip (I) the Arab. To celebrate the 1000th anniversary of the foundation of Rome in 247-248 Philip (I) the Arab staged magnificent games in which many wild beasts were exhibited in the arenas of the capital. This resulted in a series of coins featuring a gazelle, a European elk (moose), a hippopotamus, a stag, a goat (or hind), and an antelope, in addition to the lion already mentioned. The city's emblem, the she-wolf (suckling the twins Romulus and Remus), also appears as part of this series.

A few years later, during the sole reign of Gallienus (260-268), an extensive series of "animal" reverses was featured on the debased antoninianus denomination. Subsequent to this date such types appear far less frequently and are, in the main, restricted to the "legionary badge" issues of the usurpers Victorinus in Gaul and Carausius in Britannia. The charismatic Carausius also had types showing the milking of a cow, a griffin, and the traditional wolf and twins device.

In the late Empire, the wolf and twins appear on coins of another usurper, Maxentius (306-312), whose policy was to try to revive the past glories of the Imperial capital; and several decades later on small billon pieces (reduced centenionales) of the time of Constantine and his successors. In the mid 4th century a phoenix is shown on half maiorinae of Constantius II and Constans and a very fine representation of a bull, sometimes accompanied by an eagle, occurs on large billon pieces of Julian II towards the end of his short reign (360-67). One of the last animal representations on the Roman coinage is on a tiny bronze numnus of the easter Emperor Leo I (457-474) where a lion appears as a punning allusion to the emperor's name.
Historical notes:

Titus Flavius Domitianus (born on 24 October 51; given the title of Augustus on 14 September 81; died on 18 September 96)

(To the right) Bust of Domitian, sculpted by an unknown artist between 81 and 96, displayed in the Palazzo dei Conservatori in Rome

Domitian, the younger son of Vespasian and Flavia Domitilla, was born on 24 October 51. He was in Rome at the time of his father's rebellion against Vitellius in summer 69 and was lucky to escape with his life when he and his uncle, Flavius Sabinus, were besieged on the Capitol in the final days of Vitellius' regime. Although given the rank of Caesar, like his elder brother Titus, he was kept very much in the background during the 10-year reign of Vespasian. The bitter resentment that he felt over his treatment at this time and the inferiority of his status in the imperial family eventually found expression in the highly autocratic behaviour that he exhibited when he succeeded to the imperial throne on the early death of Titus in 81.

Domitian has gone down in history as one of the worst of the Roman tyrants and it is true that his memory was condemned by the Senate immediately folloncing his death. But this harsh judgement is not fully borne out by the facts and owes much to the bitter feelings of a resentful aristocracy. He certainly exhibited cruelty in dealing with those he considered to be his enemies and this increased with the discovery of plots against his regime, especially after the rebellion of Saturninus, governor of Upper Germany, in 89. However, he exhibited some aptitude as a military commander during his campaigns on the Rhine and Danube, and the frontier arrangements between the two rivers were improved. He was, in fact, the first emperor since Claudius in 43 to campaign in person. He maintained high moral standards in public performances and showed respect for religious ritual. As a builder he was tireless, restoring the temple of Jupiter on the Capitol in monumental style and adding significantly to the imperial palace on the Palatine. In financial affairs, he improved the weight and quality of the precious metal coinage and raised military pay by one-third, thereby ensuring his continued popularity with the army and the praetorian guard. The people were enter tained with frequent public spectacles and banquets, and in October 88 the Secular Games were celebrated in Rome with great pomp and ceremony. Domitian's relations with the Senate, however remained strained at best and were sometimes openly hostile. The executions of at least twelve ex-consuls are recorded during this reign, a state of affairs that led inevitably to further conspiracies and ultimately to the emperor's violent downfall. In late summer 96 a palace cabal, involving the court chamberlain and possibly even the Empress Domitia Longina, hatched a plot against the emperor's life, and on 18 September Domitian fell victim to the assassin's knife in his private apartments. Thus ended the Flavian dynasty, Rome's second imperial dynasty, which had lasted a mere twenty-seven years.

*The grades of NGC Ancients are in some cases higher than those assigned elsewhere in the marketplace. Some coins described as Choice VF (Ch VF) in an auction catalogue might be graded XF or AU by NGC Ancients if the absence of detail is caused by worn dies and/or a weak strike rather than by actual wear. In such cases, the grade is generally assigned accurately and the negative aspects of strike weakness and/or worn dies is reflected in a low score for strike quality.
